    function setAttributeOnload(object, attribute, val) {
      if(window.addEventListener) {
        window.addEventListener('load',
          function(){ object[attribute] = val; }, false);
      } else {
        window.attachEvent('onload', function(){ object[attribute] = val; });
      }
    }
  </script>

          <div class="date-outer">
        
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, May 11, 2013</span></h2>

          <div class="date-posts">
        
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry' itemprop='blogPost' itemscope='itemscope' itemtype='http://schema.org/BlogPosting'>
<meta content='http://fc05.deviantart.net/fs20/i/2007/282/7/e/Scrapyard___Tiphares_by_Desdinova84.jpg' itemprop='image_url'/>
<meta content='17232051' itemprop='blogId'/>
<meta content='7488154869658592416' itemprop='postId'/>
<a name='7488154869658592416'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title' itemprop='name'>
Science fiction novels for economists
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'><span class='post-author vcard'>
Author:
<span class='fn' itemprop='author' itemscope='itemscope' itemtype='http://schema.org/Person'>
<meta content='http://www.blogger.com/profile/09093917601641588575' itemprop='url'/>
<a class='g-profile' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/09093917601641588575' rel='author' title='author profile'>
<span itemprop='name'>Noah Smith</span>
</a>
</span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content' id='post-body-7488154869658592416' itemprop='description articleBody'>
<img alt="" height="319" src="http://fc05.deviantart.net/fs20/i/2007/282/7/e/Scrapyard___Tiphares_by_Desdinova84.jpg" title="Neill Blomkamp tooke the idea for &quot;Elysium&quot; from the Japanese manga &quot;Battle Angel Alita&quot;. Just sayin'." width="400" /><br />
<br />
Diane Coyle has a blog post called "Classics for economists," and someone on Twitter requested that I do a companion piece called "Science fiction for economists", so here it is.<br />
<br />
Really, most science fiction is about economics. What makes most future visions interesting is not just the technical particulars of the cool new Stuff, but the social ramifications. Here are some of the sci-fi books that I thought dealt with important economic issues in the most insightful and interesting ways. I also chose only books that I think are well-written, with well-conceived characters, engaging plots, and skillful writing.<br />
<br />
<br />
1. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Deepness-Sky-Zones-Thought-ebook/dp/B002H8ORKM/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368293595&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=a+deepness+in+the+sky">A Deepness in the Sky</a></i>, by Vernor Vinge<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-TVzrbTmkpfE/T6ALQvFCmPI/AAAAAAAABBs/PgjdrZKCzgE/s320/adits.jpg" width="200" /><br />
<br />
In addition to being quite possibly the best science fiction novel I've ever read, <i>Deepness</i> is also a great meditation on public economics. When Vernor Vinge became famous in the 80s, he was a hard-core libertarian - his novel <a href="http://www.amazon.com/The-Peace-War-ebook/dp/B003E74BSS/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368294015&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=the+peace+war" style="font-style: italic;">The Peace War</a>, and its sequel short story "The Ungoverned", are&nbsp;like a Real Business Cycle model come to life, with lone-wolf genius engineers teaming up with private police forces to bring down a fascist technocratic government made up of...university administrators. Ha. But by the 90s, Vinge's views on government and markets had become markedly more nuanced - in the swashbuckling space opera <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Fire-Upon-Zones-Thought-ebook/dp/B000FBJAGO/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368293922&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=a+fire+upon+the+deep">A Fire Upon the Deep</a></i>, we see private security forces failing miserably when faced with a powerful external threat (in fact, that book made me think of the "<a href="http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2011/06/libertarianism-and-tamerlane-principle.html">Tamerlane Principle</a>"). Security, Vinge realizes, is a public good.<br />
<br />
In <i>Deepness</i>, Vinge adds another public good: Research. The narrative of <i>Deepness</i> is split between a race of spider-people with roughly 20th-century technology, and a spacefaring guild of human merchants called the Qeng Ho. On the spider world, the protagonist is a brilliant scientist named Sherkaner Underhill, who is basically a Von Neumann or Feynman type. Sherkaner is the ultimate lone genius, but he ends up needing the government to fund his research. In space, meanwhile, the heroic merchant entrepreneur Pham Nuwen (who is a recurring protagonist in Vinge novels) struggles to decide whether he should turn his merchant fleet into an interstellar government. Governments, he finds, are good at producing really new scientific breakthroughs, but eventually they become unwieldy and stifle the economy and society, then collapse under their own institutional weight. The very very end of the book is - or at least, seemed to me to be - a metaphor for the Great Stagnation and the death (and future rebirth) of Big Science.<br />
<br />
<br />
2. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Makers-ebook/dp/B003VTZU1Q/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368294561&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=makers+doctorow">Makers</a></i>, by Cory Doctorow<br />
<br />
<img alt="" height="320" src="http://anjalir.files.wordpress.com/2010/01/makerslaunch2.jpg" title="See if you can identify which character is a dig at Evgeny Morozov..." width="206" /><br />
<br />
Cory Doctorow is known both for his science fiction and for being the creator of the blog <a href="http://boingboing.net/" style="font-style: italic;">Boing Boing</a>, one of the oldest and best blogs on the internet. In <i>Makers</i>, Doctorow dishes up a near future that is almost spooky in its prophetic vision. The book is all about economics, the death of corporations, the rise of freelance and temp economies, the death of old media and the rise of blogs, and the disruptive impact of technology on people's jobs. It envisions the rise of 3D printing, the startup craze (and the startup glut), and the use of intellectual property as corporations' weapon of choice to fight back against progress. It's incredibly well-written, but also extremely sad, just to warn you.<br />
<br />
<br />
3. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/The-Dispossessed-Hainish-Cycle-ebook/dp/B000FC11GA/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368294886&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=the+dispossessed">The Dispossessed</a></i>, by Ursula K. LeGuin<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://ecx.images-amazon.com/images/I/51MFm3w72TL.jpg" width="200" /><br />
<br />
It's incredibly hard to imagine a world without private property, but LeGuin pulls it off. Spoilers: A world without property is pretty boring and fairly poor. But LeGuin also shows us another world, much like our own, where the anti-property anarcho-syndicalist movement was suppressed and tamed, much like Marxism was suppressed and tamed here on Earth. What's interesting is that although anarcho-syndicalism doesn't work incredibly well on the world where it's implemented, the anarcho-syndicalist <i>idea</i> and <i>movement</i> serve as a sort of permanent opposition force on the capitalist world. When <a href="http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/04/book-review-occupy-handbook.html">I read Robert M. Buckley</a> writing that Marxism has fulfilled a similar role in the West here on Earth, I immediately thought of <i>The Dispossessed</i>.<br />
<br />
<br />
4. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Down-Out-Magic-Kingdom-ebook/dp/B008TGL0EM/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368295295&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=down+and+out+in+the+magic+kingdom">Down and Out in the Magic Kingdom</a></i>, by Cory Doctorow<br />
<br />
<img alt="" height="320" src="http://www.sfreviews.net/large_covers/down_and_out_mk.jpg" title="It's not a museum, it's a ride." width="220" /><br />
<br />
Doctorow again. In this book, he examines what a true post-scarcity society would look like. Spoiler: It looks a lot like a bunch of sarcastic bohemian Canadian people. But basically, I think that's probably what the future <i>will</i> look like, at least if we're lucky. Anyway, this book is notable for the concept of "whuffie", an online currency based on peer approval, which arguably inspired Facebook's "like" button.<br />
<br />
<br />
5. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Rainbows-End-ebook/dp/B004M8SR2O/ref=sr_1_1?s=digital-text&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368297694&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=rainbows+end">Rainbows End</a></i>, by Vernor Vinge<br />
<br />
<img alt="" height="320" src="http://www.ugotrade.com/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/05/VernorVinge_RainbowsEnd.jpg" title="And if you really love AR, check out the anime Denno Coil." width="209" /><br />
<br />
Vinge again. <i>Rainbows End</i> is a sad, thoughtful novel about old age and obsolescence (notice that there is no apostrophe in the title). But it's also one of the most visionary novels about future labor markets. In an interconnected world in which skills never stay fresh for long and most value is created through entertainment, old engineers have to go back to high school, and new corporations are started by teenagers collaborating online with strangers halfway around the world. <i>Rainbows End</i> is also famous for envisioning the technology of Augmented Reality; this novel probably inspired Google Glass. Interestingly, Vinge continues his evolution toward a balanced view of public goods, adding education to the list of things that government needs to provide. (<u>Update</u>: In an email, Vinge points out that he never specified that the high school in <i>Rainbows End</i> was government-funded! Touche!)<br />
<br />
<br />
6. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Accelerando-ebook/dp/B000OIZUC6/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368295477&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=accelerando">Accelerando</a></i>, by Charles Stross<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://www.sfsite.com/gra/0510/aclg.jpg" width="211" /><br />
<br />
Charles Stross, another <a href="http://www.antipope.org/charlie/">noted blogger</a>, loves to play with ideas, even if he doesn't believe in them. In <i>Accelerando</i>, he mainly plays with the idea of the Singularity, but he also plays with a bunch of far-out funky future economics. In one part, the main character, impresario and wandering entrepreneur Manfred Macx, uses advanced computer algorithms to successfully implement an optimal centrally planned economy, by predicting what humans will want before they even know they want it. Macx's various disruptive innovations inevitably draw the ire of the law, and he creates a protective cloud of AI lawyers to wage constant "lawfare" against governments and corporations alike. In another part of the book, the entire solar system is taken over by sentient High-Frequency Trading algorithms. But I haven't really spoiled the book for you, since these are only about 0.1% of the ideas contained within. Note: Stross has also written a series called the <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Family-Trade-Merchant-Princes-ebook/dp/B004DNWBZ2/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368296837&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=stross+merchant+princes">Merchant Princes</a> series that deals even more with economics, but I haven't read it.<br />
<br />
<br />
7. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Lucifers-Hammer-ebook/dp/B004478DOU/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368295806&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=lucifer%27s+hammer">Lucifer's Hammer</a></i>, by Larry Niven and Jerry Pournelle<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://ecx.images-amazon.com/images/I/51A1b0iz%2BjL.jpg" width="194" /><br />
<br />
<i>Lucifer's Hammer</i> is a story about a comet hitting Earth, and the aftermath. It's notable for its quaint Reaganite conservative politics (it came out in 1977), and does make a couple of glaring economic mistakes (for example, a guy trying to build a nuclear power plant is an independent wildcatting entrepreneur instead of a giant government-backed corporation). But it makes up for that with its excellent portrayal of what the economy would be like in the immediate aftermath of an abrupt civilizational collapse. Hint: Farming, containment of contagious diseases, de-specialization of labor, and collective security become very very important.<br />
<br />
<br />
8. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/The-Windup-Girl-ebook/dp/B006TKP2B2/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368296093&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=the+windup+girl">The Windup Girl</a></i>, by Paolo Bacigalupi<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/books/1278940608l/6597651.jpg" width="209" /><br />
<br />
Brutally dark and hopeless, <i>The Windup Girl</i> is a book about peak oil, global civilizational decline, and the (temporary) end of positive-sum economies. In a suddenly overpopulated world, humans are forced into a constant Hobbesian zero-sum game, and most moral norms go right out the window. Warning: This is very tough book to read. But it serves as an important reminder of the Malthusian menace that forever lurks just outside the circle of light provided by the flickering candle-flame of modern technology.<br />
<br />
<br />
9. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Moon-Harsh-Mistress-Robert-Heinlein/dp/0312863551/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368296316&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=the+moon+is+a+harsh+mistress">The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</a></i>, by Robert Heinlein<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://ecx.images-amazon.com/images/I/410BnoLMnoL.jpg" width="213" /><br />
<br />
Actually a mythic retelling of the American Revolution, <i>The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</i> contains some very interesting thoughts on colonialism and the Resource Curse. Unsurprisingly, terrorism is used as a way to make resource colonialism too expensive for the occupying power. Unfortunately, we don't get to see the political struggles and despotic regimes that probably arose in the aftermath of lunar independence. But Heinlein also does use the book to play with some interesting libertarian ideas, like a privatized court system.<br />
<br />
<br />
10. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Schismatrix-Plus-Complete-Shapers-Mechanists-Universe/dp/0441003702/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368296633&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=schismatrix">Schismatrix</a></i>, by Bruce Sterling<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://www.coverbrowser.com/image/bestselling-sci-fi-fantasy-2007/2095-1.jpg" width="208" /><br />
<br />
Simply one of the most wide-ranging and visionary science fiction novels ever written. Bruce Sterling is like an eternally erupting quasar of creativity, and this is his finest book. None of the economics here makes any sense - or, more accurately, it all takes place in such a funky, crazy future world that it's impossible to know if it makes any sense.<br />
<br />
<br />
11. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Permutation-City-ebook/dp/B004JHY84E/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368296874&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=permutation+city">Permutation City</a></i>, by Greg Egan<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://www.kurzweilai.net/images/permutation-city.jpg" width="196" /><br />
<br />
If there's any sci-fi novel that beats <i>Schismatrix</i> for far-out blow-your-brain-out-the-back-of-your-skull vision, it's <i>Permutation City</i>. If I hadn't already had the idea for <a href="http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2012/08/desire-modification-ultimate-technology.html">D-Mod</a>, this book (written over a decade before I thought of the concept) would have given it to me fully formed. <i>Permutation City </i>is about the ultimate nature and purpose of human consciousness and experience, and yet it has implications for technologies that are being developed right now, as we speak.<br />
<br />
<br />
12. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Reamde-ebook/dp/B004XVN0WW/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368297310&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=reamde">Reamde</a></i>, by Neal Stephenson<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://www.wired.com/geekdad/wp-content/uploads/2011/09/reamde.jpg" width="211" /><br />
<br />
Most people would recommend Stephenson's <i>Snow Crash</i> or <i>Cryptonomicon</i>, but for economics I like <i>Reamde</i> the best. Although not technically sci-fi, it has that flavor. The hero is an aging tech entrepreneur who owns a game that's a combination of World of Warcraft and Bitcoin (yes, this book predicts Bitcoin). It also deals with the economic incentives of the Russian mob, the challenges facing smart young tech workers in China and Hungary, and lots of other cool features of today's global economy. It's not Stephenson's #1 awesomest book, though; that would be <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Anathem-ebook/dp/B0015DPXKI/ref=sr_1_1?s=digital-text&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368297537&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=anathem">Anathem</a></i>.<br />
<br />
<br />
13. <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Game-Thrones-Song-Fire-ebook/dp/B000QCS8TW/ref=sr_1_1?s=digital-text&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1368298594&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=game+of+thrones">The <i>Game of Thrones</i> series</a>, by George R.R. Martin<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://cdn.hbowatch.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/01/A-Game-of-Thrones-book-Cover-NOvel.jpg" width="209" /><br />
<br />
Actually, I lied earlier...the person who requested a "Sci-fi for economists" list also asked me to include fantasy. But the <i>Game of Thrones</i> books (actually called A Song of Ice and Fire, though few use that name anymore) are really the only fantasy novels I can think of that deal with economics in an interesting way. You get to see a lot of the messed-up economies of medieval times, including feudalism, slavery, anarchy, blood sport, and the difficulty of international trade with poor information and unreliable transportation.<br />
<br />
<br />
Anyway, there's my list. I haven't read everything out there, obviously - I hear that Ken MacLeod's books have a lot of economics in them, for instance, as well as some of Heinlein's other works. But if you're in the econ field and you want to think big deep thoughts about economics under different technological and social conditions, these books are for you. They're also a lot of fun.<br />
<br />
<u>Update</u>: Mark Palko looks at "<a href="http://observationalepidemiology.blogspot.com/2013/05/thrillers-on-economics-updated.html">Crime novels for economists</a>". Diane, looks like you started a meme!<br />
<br />
<u>Update 2</u>: Other sci-fi recommendations via <a href="http://krugman.blogs.nytimes.com/2013/05/11/more-science-fiction-for-economists-seriously-time-wasting/">Paul Krugman</a> and <a href="http://www.forbes.com/sites/timworstall/2013/05/12/science-fiction-and-fantasy-to-learn-economics-from/">Tim Worstall</a>.<br />
<br />
Update 3: A couple of additions...<br />
<br />
<br />
14. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Year-Flood-Margaret-Atwood/dp/0307455475/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1369891862&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=the+year+of+the+flood">The Year of the Flood</a></i>, by Margaret Atwood<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_-PUz1DMj-Oc/SwxIKjUMqrI/AAAAAAAACCU/5hY9PjnlgqI/s320/the-year-of-the-flood.jpg" width="211" /><br />
<br />
I had a very good reason for omitting this book from the original list: I hadn't read it yet. Now I have. <i>The Year of the Flood</i> is the companion novel to <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Oryx-Crake-Margaret-Atwood/dp/0385721676/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1369893708&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=oryx+and+crake">Oryx and Crake</a></i>, a philosophical dystopia/apocalypse novel (and one of the best such novels ever written, IMHO). But you can read <i>The Year of the Flood</i> on its own. This book is, basically, about a market dystopia. Everything is for sale in this future, and yet negative externalities, asymmetric information, and weak institutions make the world a nightmare.<br />
<br />
<br />
15. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/When-Gravity-Fails-George-Effinger/dp/0765313588/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1369891919&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=when+gravity+fails">When Gravity Fails</a></i>, by George Alec Effinger<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/WhenGravityFailsBantamSpectra1988.jpg/220px-WhenGravityFailsBantamSpectra1988.jpg" width="192" /><br />
<br />
Don't know why I forgot this one earlier. It's basically a novel about underground economies and organized crime. Interesting for those who like to think about the economics of such things. Also, an extremely well-written and fun book.<br />
<br />
<br />
16. <i><a href="http://www.amazon.com/Stand-Zanzibar-John-Brunner/dp/0765326787/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1369892270&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=stand+on+zanzibar">Stand on Zanzibar</a></i>, by John Bruenner<br />
<br />
<img height="320" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/StandOnZanzibar(1stEd).jpg/200px-StandOnZanzibar(1stEd).jpg" width="213" /><br />
<br />
A lot of commenters have been mentioning this one, and they're absolutely right. <i>Stand on Zanzibar</i> is a little dated, but it really picks up on a lot of economic trends that were just starting in the 60s (e.g. the pricing of previously plentiful commodities such as water). The economics are all about the effects of overpopulation, which seems less menacing in the 2010s, but is still a major problem in parts of the world. A great futurist novel from the past.<br />
<br />
<br />
<u>Note</u>: Still no <i>Foundation</i> series. I love the <i>Foundation</i> series, and it's really fun, but the economics are crappy (agglomeration economies encourage the collapse of empires?). And more importantly, the whole conceit of psychohistory is very dangerous for economists; our models will never forecast that well, the Universe is stochastic rather than deterministic, and we should avoid the temptation for intellectual hubris. &nbsp;Foundation is social science's "physics envy" writ large. We are not, and will never be, Hari Seldon, so <i>Foundation</i>, classic as it is, doesn't go on my list!
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

The plot point seemed based on a common misconception that ancient precious metal coins were supposed to have a constant value, that debasing was cheating. However, in the real world folks were greedy gits who would shave slivers off the sides of coins. Thus the metal content would no longer match the face value of the coin. The sociological and economic aspects are quite explicit and well thought through.  On top of that, a fictional Martian historian toward the end of the 3rd book presents an overarching theory of history that is as good as anything I know of from any real historian.  Specifically, that each phase of history is a struggle between the phase that precedes it and the one that follows it.  In our case, that means a struggle between the oligarchy of the Middle Ages and the true democracy that is achieved on Mars later in this century. Iron Council, for instance, is basically a mythologizing of a labor revolt, plus giant monsters and baleful magicks.\74br /\76\74br /\76Daniel Abraham\46#39;s Long Price deals extensively with the political and economic consequences of its system of magic: beings created from thought which embody a specific concept.                 comment.link = comment.permalink = entry.link[2].href;
              }
              if (entry.link[3]) {
                var pid = /.*comments\/default\/(\d+)\?.*/.exec(entry.link[3].href);
                if (pid && pid[1]) {
                  comment.parentId = pid[1];
                }
              }
            }
            comment.deleteclass = 'item-control blog-admin';
            if (entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
              for (var k in entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
                if (entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].name == 'blogger.itemClass') {
                  comment.deleteclass += ' ' + entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].value;
                } else if (entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].name == 'blogger.displayTime') {
                  comment.displayTime = entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].value;
                }
              }
            }
            comments.push(comment);
          }
        }
        return comments;
      };

      var paginator = function(callback) {
        if (hasMore()) {
          var url = config.feed + '?alt=json&v=2&orderby=published&reverse=false&max-results=50';
          if (cursor) {
            url += '&published-min=' + new Date(cursor).toISOString();
          }
          window.bloggercomments = function(data) {
            var parsed = parse(data);
            cursor = parsed.length < 50 ? null
                : parseInt(parsed[parsed.length - 1].timestamp) + 1
            callback(parsed);
            window.bloggercomments = null;
          }
          url += '&callback=bloggercomments';
          var script = document.createElement('script');
          script.type = 'text/javascript';
          script.src = url;
          document.getElementsByTagName('head')[0].appendChild(script);
        }
      };
      var hasMore = function() {
        return !!cursor;
      };
      var getMeta = function(key, comment) {
        if ('iswriter' == key) {
          var matches = !!comment.author
              && comment.author.name == config.authorName
              && comment.author.profileUrl == config.authorUrl;
          return matches ? 'true' : '';
        } else if ('deletelink' == key) {
          return config.baseUri + '/delete-comment.g?blogID='
               + config.blogId + '&postID=' + comment.id;
        } else if ('deleteclass' == key) {
          return comment.deleteclass;
        }
        return '';
      };

      var replybox = null;
      var replyUrlParts = null;
      var replyParent = undefined;

      var onReply = function(commentId, domId) {
        if (replybox == null) {
          // lazily cache replybox, and adjust to suit this style:
          replybox = document.getElementById('comment-editor');
          if (replybox != null) {
            replybox.height = '250px';
            replybox.style.display = 'block';
            replyUrlParts = replybox.src.split('#');
          }
        }
        if (replybox && (commentId !== replyParent)) {
          document.getElementById(domId).insertBefore(replybox, null);
          replybox.src = replyUrlParts[0]
              + (commentId ? '&parentID=' + commentId : '')
              + '#' + replyUrlParts[1];
          replyParent = commentId;
        }
      };

      var hash = (window.location.hash || '#').substring(1);
      var startThread, targetComment;
      if (/^comment-form_/.test(hash)) {
        startThread = hash.substring('comment-form_'.length);
      } else if (/^c[0-9]+$/.test(hash)) {
        targetComment = hash.substring(1);
      }

      // Configure commenting API:
      var configJso = {
        'maxDepth': config.maxThreadDepth
      };
      var provider = {
        'id': config.postId,
        'data': items,
        'loadNext': paginator,
        'hasMore': hasMore,
        'getMeta': getMeta,
        'onReply': onReply,
        'rendered': true,
        'initComment': targetComment,
        'initReplyThread': startThread,
        'config': configJso,
        'messages': msgs
      };

      var render = function() {
        if (window.goog && window.goog.comments) {
          var holder = document.getElementById('comment-holder');
          window.goog.comments.render(holder, provider);
        }
      };

      // render now, or queue to render when library loads:
      if (window.goog && window.goog.comments) {
        render();
      } else {
        window.goog = window.goog || {};
        window.goog.comments = window.goog.comments || {};
        window.goog.comments.loadQueue = window.goog.comments.loadQueue || [];
        window.goog.comments.loadQueue.push(render);
      }
    })();
// ]]>
</script>

I&#39;d guess that AR might have appeared in science fiction before these points, but I don&#39;t know of any early stories about it.</p><span id='bc_0_7MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' class='item-control blog-admin pid-544349192'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=7683735369757481498'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_11BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_11B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_5I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c540578945703760215' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_13M' Nixon stand-ins appear in RFA, Valis, Flow My Tears, and other places. Certain economic ideas do pop up in Dick&#39;s work--in Galactic Pot Healer, government money is worthless and only old silver coins have value. In Ubik, cash is replaced by credit cards and AI-driven appliances demand micropayments for every service. Dick clearly valued the work of tradesmen, craftsmen, and artisans, and distrusted faceless corporate entities. <br /><br />But on the whole, I&#39;d have to also mention The Space Merchants as possibly the best SF novel about capitalism.</p><span id='bc_0_37MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-768738766'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=2078543042897025304'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_37BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_37B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_37I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Reply</a></div></ol><div id='bc_0_37T_box' class='comment-replybox-thread'></div></div></span></div><div id='bc_0_38B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_41B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c1934603319062840669' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_41M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/09190806399593789816'>nathanbp</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368319242171#c1934603319062840669'>8:40 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_41MC' class='comment-content'>For economic fantasy I suggest Spice and Wolf, a Japanese novel series (published in English by Yen Press) where the main character is a merchant in a fantasy setting. Plot points include, among others, currency exchange rates and speculation, smuggling, and manipulating commodity prices.</p><span id='bc_0_41MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1517351608'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=1934603319062840669'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_41BR' class='comment-replies'><span id='bc_0_41b+seedI5ktD' kind='d'><div id='bc_0_39T' class='comment-thread inline-thread' kind='t'   t='0' u='0'><span id='bc_0_39TT' class='thread-toggle thread-expanded' kind='g'><span id='bc_0_39TA' class='thread-arrow'></span><span id='bc_0_39TN' class='thread-count'><span id='bc_0_39TNT' style='display: none;'></span><span id='bc_0_39TNU' style='display: none;'></span><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Replies</a><div id='bc_0_39TD' class='thread-dropContainer thread-expanded'><span class='thread-drop'></span></div></span></span><ol id='bc_0_39TC' class='thread-chrome thread-expanded'><div><li id='bc_0_39B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c9182064812437502816' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_39M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/13321329197063620556'>Damien Sullivan</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368327036629#c9182064812437502816'>10:50 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_39MC' class='comment-content'>I am scooped!  But yes.  It&#39;s one of the few works in any genre where people would think of economics in the description spontaneously, without having to stretch.  <br /><br />I also second Pohl and Kornbluth&#39;s _The Space Merchants_, and maybe Merchants of Venus.  SF about advertising, not exactly irrelevant.<br /><br />Clarke: I think _Imperial Earth_, _The Deep Range_, and _Earthlight_ all have economics issues.  As much so as Game of Thrones, anyway.<br /><br />_Taran Wanderer_ had Taran learning about various crafts, which is something.  The later Hodgell novels include a B-plot of &quot;how do I feed my people?&quot;  I think economics wandered in and out of the Pern novels; at least, one doesn&#39;t have to wonder how the Weyrs feed themselves, and the characters worry about job obsolescence.</p><span id='bc_0_39MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-220020329'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=9182064812437502816'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_39BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_39B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_40B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c1634958820497870463' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_40M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368427577229#c1634958820497870463'>2:46 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_40MC' class='comment-content'>It wasn&#39;t about exchange rates per se, but that they were minting somewhat debased coins and passing them off as good as the old ones. The plot point seemed based on a common misconception that ancient precious metal coins were supposed to have a constant value, that debasing was cheating. However, in the real world folks were greedy gits who would shave slivers off the sides of coins. Thus the metal content would no longer match the face value of the coin. This is of course the equivalent of debasing coins already in circulation. It also caused headaches for mints because of Gresham&#39;s law: the bad drive out the good, in other words the new coins will be hoarded and the old ever-shrinking coins remain in circulation. So it became common practice to debase newly minted coins after a certain time had passed. <br /><br />For a more wonkish explanation of how it worked you can read fr.ex. this: <br />http://www.voxeu.org/article/medieval-monetary-practices</p><span id='bc_0_40MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1504112498'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=1634958820497870463'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_40BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_40B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_39I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Reply</a></div></ol><div id='bc_0_39T_box' class='comment-replybox-thread'></div></div></span></div><div id='bc_0_41B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_42B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-kkH6XfYL_HQ/Td0p5HsQs_I/AAAAAAAAAC4/JRScZ_W4buY/s45/Green%25252BRiver%25252BUtah.JPG'></img></div><div id='c5256162311114822315' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_42M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/05741738070067590221'>Brett</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368321211048#c5256162311114822315'>9:13 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_42MC' class='comment-content'>I re-read <em>Deepness</em> and loved it. That one should really be read for anyone curious as to what a technologically &quot;mature&quot; civilization that would be recognizably human to us would look like. It can be rather depressing - Vinge&#39;s book is the only SF novel I&#39;ve ever read that managed to make a setting consisting of hundreds of colonies scattered across hundreds of light-years (with travel at one-third the speed of light) seem <em>claustrophic</em>. A big part of that story is showing how the forces that shape society can ultimately be beyond even the extraordinary capabilities of someone like Pham Nuwen, and how we respond when we are finally forced to face that. <br /><br />It also featured augmented reality stuff, although it&#39;s treated as banal, common-place technology because that&#39;s what it is in-universe. They have heads-up-displays that overlay what they&#39;re seeing with &quot;Layers&quot; and interactive displays. </p><span id='bc_0_42MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-504608560'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=5256162311114822315'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_42BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_42B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_44B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c5442966931772711638' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_44M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/07345644942695543499'>nycgeoff</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368322232539#c5442966931772711638'>9:30 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_44MC' class='comment-content'>This is a good list (The first book I thought of when I read the title was Rainbows End). A couple of other ideas:<br /><br />Ian McDonald&#39;s &quot;River of Gods&quot; is a good look at a developed India<br /><br />&quot;Nekropolis&quot; by Maureen McHugh looks at a possible future North Africa in what seems to me to be a believable way.</p><span id='bc_0_44MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span id='bc_0_49MC' class='comment-content'>You say that LeGuin pulled off a world without property, and that it&#39;s pretty boring. But Anares is an impoverished world, where life would be marginal under any conceivable economy. So, confounded factors. In that sense, LeGuin set herself too easy a task, much as I admire The Dispossessed. How would anarchy have played out on Urras?</p><span id='bc_0_49MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1332301431'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=7786499440446225860'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_49BR' class='comment-replies'><span id='bc_0_49b+seedI5kvD' kind='d'><div id='bc_0_46T' class='comment-thread inline-thread' kind='t'   t='0' u='0'><span id='bc_0_46TT' class='thread-toggle thread-expanded' kind='g'><span id='bc_0_46TA' class='thread-arrow'></span><span id='bc_0_46TN' class='thread-count'><span id='bc_0_46TNT' style='display: none;'></span><span id='bc_0_46TNU' style='display: none;'></span><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Replies</a><div id='bc_0_46TD' class='thread-dropContainer thread-expanded'><span class='thread-drop'></span></div></span></span><ol id='bc_0_46TC' class='thread-chrome thread-expanded'><div><li id='bc_0_46B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c3040904113227837257' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_46M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/13321329197063620556'>Damien Sullivan</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368327427192#c3040904113227837257'>10:57 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_46MC' class='comment-content'>Part of it is that no one wanted to take Anarres away from the anarchists.  Could an anarchist Urras have defended itself from a capitalist Anarres?  More accurately, the anarchists were given freedom and independence on an otherwise unoccupied moon.</p><span id='bc_0_46MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-220020329'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=3040904113227837257'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_46BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_46B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_47B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c3106039028742715503' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_47M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368366767024#c3106039028742715503'>9:52 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_47MC' class='comment-content'>As you gentleman point out here, Anares being a barely livable world is the key to the viability of whole society. The ongoing Man against Nature struggle is what provided the ongoing catalyst for the cooperative interaction needed to sustain an anarchist society. But I think what Noah isn&#39;t getting in his description, is that a major theme of the book is how much more human and fulfilling that simpler society was than Capitalist Urras. You don&#39;t have to agree with this idea of course, to enjoy the book. </p><span id='bc_0_47MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1568069185'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=3106039028742715503'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_47BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_47B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_48B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c3316106284059347173' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_48M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Nathanael</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1369733422682#c3316106284059347173'>5:30 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_48MC' class='comment-content'>As I note below, the thing I found most interesting about The Dispossessed is that -- although Annares is clearly a better society to live in than Urras -- Annares is naturally degenerating into bureaucracy and property.  It is very hard to maintain the society of Annares.  Even with universal education from early childhood.  <br /><br />The main plot of half of the book is about the Syndicate of Initiative trying to do *exactly what the philosophy of Annares claims they should be doing*... and being fought at every turn by what can only be called the government of Annares, which isn&#39;t even supposed to exist.  And getting badmouthed by the majority of the population.<br /><br />In short, although LeGuin makes it clear that the society of Annares is a better and happier society to live in, *it isn&#39;t stable in any way*.</p><span id='bc_0_48MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1338132252'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=3316106284059347173'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_48BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_48B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_46I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Reply</a></div></ol><div id='bc_0_46T_box' class='comment-replybox-thread'></div></div></span></div><div id='bc_0_49B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_51B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c7531198906546835894' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_51M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368333760260#c7531198906546835894'>12:42 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_51MC' class='comment-content'>Off topic, but my first exposure to Charles Stross came courtesy of Paul Krugman, who recommended Stross&#39;s Laundry Files on his blog. class='comment-content'>I&#39;d second Accelerando, Permutation City and the stunning Schismatrix (Doctorow is on my shelf of shame) but I&#39;d like to make a bid for adding Frank Herbert&#39;s Dune. While it has some of the trapings of medieval fantasy, these are a consequence of the abandonment machine intelligence in the Butlerian Jihad thousands of years before the events of the novel take place. If you junk AI and repose almost all significant skills in very rare human specialists like the &quot;mentats&quot; (human computers) a guild system supported by a feudal political order is one way in which organizations that invest in this labour power can reap their investment. </p><span id='bc_0_78MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-381088369'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=7402145737450695101'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_78BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_78B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_79B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c524531313930520503' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_79M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368376159699#c524531313930520503'>12:29 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_79MC' class='comment-content'>Not sure where we put Frederick Pohl and Cyril Kornbluth<br /><br />&#39;The Space Merchants&#39;<br /><br />and &#39;Gladiators at Law&#39;<br /><br />and also Kornbluth&#39;s &#39;The Syndic&#39;<br /><br />But TSM is about a world ruled by advertising agencies, where the logic of ever increasing consumption gets them into deep, deep trouble.  Gladiators at Law has a world run by supercorporations and if you lose your job, things get very grim indeed.  The Syndic has America run by the Mafia, with the government agents trying to get back into power.<br /><br />Not so much explorations of new economics, but logical extensions of where social trends (as seen in the 1950s) was taking us.<br /><br />As someone said of The Space Merchants &#39;it was supposed to be satirical, but as the world it depicts has become more and more true, the humor has lessened&#39;.<br /><br />Valuethinker<br /></p><span id='bc_0_79MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-2063901767'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=524531313930520503'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_79BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_79B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_81B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c566321185291507852' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_81M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/10137289746215393056'>bbuc</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368376879522#c566321185291507852'>12:41 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_81MC' class='comment-content'>Thanks for this. The industrial revolution tajes place in Kerala. Wonderful, educating book</p><span id='bc_0_82MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1961519585'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=4578283705834942821'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_82BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_82B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_85B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c2707066532347024885' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_85M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/05147005081906046351'>Quasit</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368382078337#c2707066532347024885'>2:07 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_85MC' class='comment-content'>Mack Reynolds brought economics to science fiction in a big way. He explored the implications of a world in which technology had eliminated the need for the majority of the population to work.<br /><br />He also wrote several memorable stories about marketing and packaging, specifically about wrappers on bars of soap.<br /><br />His work was not high art, I suppose. It was very readable, but he wrote quickly and at times included a bit of soft-core pornography in some of his novels. I doubt that any of it is still in print today.<br /><br />Still, it&#39;s well worth looking up.</p><span id='bc_0_85MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-579975353'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=2707066532347024885'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_85BR' class='comment-replies'><span id='bc_0_85b+seedI5lWD' kind='d'><div id='bc_0_83T' class='comment-thread inline-thread' kind='t'   t='0' u='0'><span id='bc_0_83TT' class='thread-toggle thread-expanded' kind='g'><span id='bc_0_83TA' class='thread-arrow'></span><span id='bc_0_83TN' class='thread-count'><span id='bc_0_83TNT' style='display: none;'></span><span id='bc_0_83TNU' style='display: none;'></span><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Replies</a><div id='bc_0_83TD' class='thread-dropContainer thread-expanded'><span class='thread-drop'></span></div></span></span><ol id='bc_0_83TC' class='thread-chrome thread-expanded'><div><li id='bc_0_83B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c2972793698877077602' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_83M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368418195261#c2972793698877077602'>12:09 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_83MC' class='comment-content'>I&#39;ll second that.  He wrote in his 1973 redux of the Edward Bellamy futuristic classic, _Looking Backward, From the Year 2000_, &quot;These super-corporations finally squeezed out just about every other enterprise in the country.... You might say the corporations...became the new government. If you can call it a government.&quot;  His sequel, _Commune 2000_, dealt with the impact of a Universal Guaranteed Income.</p><span id='bc_0_83MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-72614251'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=2972793698877077602'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_83BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_83B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_84B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c4421586152475498810' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_84M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368568213493#c4421586152475498810'>5:50 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_84MC' class='comment-content'>It is worth knowing that Mack Reynolds ran as the VP for the Socialist Party of America (although his Wikipedia entry doesn&#39;t mention that).<br /><br />There was an Ace Double &#39;Code Duello&#39; and &#39;Computer War&#39;.<br /><br />Code Duello is about a planet so obsessed with national security against &#39;the Englistes&#39; a revolutionary terrorist organization that seems to have no members.  A team of interplanetary agents (a hotpotch of truly eccentric and problematic special agents) is sent in to try to help them....<br /><br />Computer War is about a society very thinly disguised version of the modern USA (say the USA in 2020) being pinned down in a war with a low technology society ....<br /><br />Both are massive satires of the US of the mid 1960s.<br /><br />Both have quite interesting economics themes. <br /><br />valuethinker</p><span id='bc_0_84MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-2063901767'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=4421586152475498810'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_84BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_84B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_83I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Reply</a></div></ol><div id='bc_0_83T_box' class='comment-replybox-thread'></div></div></span></div><div id='bc_0_85B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_88B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c2193797978611722672' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_88M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.lagrange.edu/academics/political-science/faculty/tracy-lightcap.html'>Tracy Lightcap</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368387746839#c2193797978611722672'>3:42 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_88MC' class='comment-content'>Ok, decent choices, but &#8230;<br /><br />No Brave New World? If there was ever a scifi novel about economics - and about the unanticipated consequences of solving economic problems - that tops Huxley&#39;s classic, I&#39;ve never read it. A truly great book.<br /><br />No Norstrilia? The premise of this book is one of the most engaging in scifi and is great for economic thinking: what if a poor planet of sheep farmers discover that the disease that infects their sheep causes them to extrude a saliva that prevents aging? And, btw, promotes telepathic powers among the colonists themselves? And they become the richest people in the galaxy, but their government cuts them off from interstellar trade and overvalues the currency so much that everyone is reduced to a life style of a sheep station in OZ about 1950? And the galaxy is ruled by philosopher-kings? And a lot of the work is done by genetically altered animals (the &quot;Underpeople&quot;)? And &#8230; well, you get the idea. This is probably the single most imaginative piece of scifi every written and the only scifi novel I&#39;ve ever read that give you a sense of real disconnect from the present. <br /><br />No Stranger in a Strange Land? If you have Moon is a Harsh Mistress in here, Heinlein&#39;s masterpiece deserves a place too. No need to describe this one, I guess; anyone who&#39;s read it knows how innovative the economic ideas are.<br /></p><span id='bc_0_88MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-861736435'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=2193797978611722672'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_88BR' class='comment-replies'><span id='bc_0_88b+seedI5lQD' kind='d'><div id='bc_0_86T' class='comment-thread inline-thread' kind='t'   t='0' u='0'><span id='bc_0_86TT' class='thread-toggle thread-expanded' kind='g'><span id='bc_0_86TA' class='thread-arrow'></span><span id='bc_0_86TN' class='thread-count'><span id='bc_0_86TNT' style='display: none;'></span><span id='bc_0_86TNU' style='display: none;'></span><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Replies</a><div id='bc_0_86TD' class='thread-dropContainer thread-expanded'><span class='thread-drop'></span></div></span></span><ol id='bc_0_86TC' class='thread-chrome thread-expanded'><div><li id='bc_0_86B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c6119916610923330348' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_86M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.lagrange.edu/academics/political-science/faculty/tracy-lightcap.html'>Tracy Lightcap</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368388342312#c6119916610923330348'>3:52 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_86MC' class='comment-content'>I forgot to mention that Rod MacBain, the main character in Norstrilla, arbitrages his great wealth to buy the Earth (yes, the whole planet, lock, stock, and barrel). class='comment-content'>Ooooh, I just started reading it! Sounds familiar?</p><span id='bc_0_99MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-898249902'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=525514303684192612'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_99BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_99B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_100B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='c2294655861895148749' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_100M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'>Anonymous</cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368460185212#c2294655861895148749'>11:49 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_100MC' class='comment-content'>What about Iain M. Banks&#39; Culture?<br />It can be read as a sort of &#8220;computer-aided&#8221; post-scarcity regime. See: <br />Yannick Rumpala, Artificial intelligences and political organization: an exploration based on the science fiction work of Iain M. Banks, Technology in Society, Volume 34, Issue 1, 2012, http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0160791X11000728<br /></p><span id='bc_0_100MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-845055232'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=2294655861895148749'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_100BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_100B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_101B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c5537581633302663478' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_101M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/15851195163937411247'>JessicaYogini</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368460523830#c5537581633302663478'>11:55 AM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_101MC' class='comment-content'>I have to third the recommendation for the Mars trilogy.  The sociological and economic aspects are quite explicit and well thought through.  On top of that, a fictional Martian historian toward the end of the 3rd book presents an overarching theory of history that is as good as anything I know of from any real historian.  Specifically, that each phase of history is a struggle between the phase that precedes it and the one that follows it.  In our case, that means a struggle between the oligarchy of the Middle Ages and the true democracy that is achieved on Mars later in this century.  Thus, our political structures have democratic elections, but the economy (and through it everything that surrounds elections too) are controlled oligarchically.<br />Kim Stanley Robinson&#39;s &quot;The Years of Rice and Salt&quot; does not have so much economics per se, but its portrayal of world history as it might have been if the Black Death had completely depopulated Europe will be interesting to at least some who find Noah&#39;s list of interest.  He also has a new novel 2312, which seems to be an extension of the Mars Trilogy, but which I haven&#39;t read yet.<br />Noah, thank you for the list.  No criticism intended by these additions.  Fortunately, we live in an age when no one could possibly read everything, or even just all the good books.  This condition itself is actually fairly rare in history.<br />And yes, Diamond Age by Neal Stephenson<br />And it is just a piece of the book, but Altered Carbon by Richard K. Morgan explores what how people might deal with it if you could be put into a new young clone of yourself when you get old, but the process is obscenely expensive.  <br /></p><span id='bc_0_101MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1521942575'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=5537581633302663478'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_101BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_101B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_102B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c8559812186227696209' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_102M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/17363056735449101298'>Benedict Treloar</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368465645623#c8559812186227696209'>1:20 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_102MC' class='comment-content'>I&#39;d disagree with your comment around &quot;the only fantasy series...&quot;. Terry Pratchett&#39;s Discworld series, a satirical comic-fantasy series is incredibly well written and plays with a lot of economic concepts, many micro-related. Thud! and the Moist von Lipwig series in particular address genuinely interesting micro problems, The Fifth Element spends a lot of time looking at regulation, and the Last Continent features an interesting take on the concept of fiat money. The series has a somewhat British tone, but should be fairly accessible.<br /><br />Additionally, KJ Parker&#39;s &quot;Devices and Desires&quot; trilogy (which I&#39;ll admit sounds a bit EL James), provides a better exposition of medieaval-era economics than Game of Thrones. Set in a world dominated by a manufacturing-based control economy, which dominates a set of well-described feudal systems, you also see later in the books a great description of nomadic merchant economies. In addition to being a fascinating read, it&#39;s also the best conceived fantasy work I&#39;ve ever read - once I had got through the first half of the book, I was completely hooked.<br /><br />If you&#39;re looking for a shorter introduction to the pseudonymic author (who is apparently well recognised in an other genre under their real name), I&#39;d recommend Purple and Black, which contains less economics, but uses a relatively unusual written form to describe the life of a major general in a Rome-like world.<br /><br />Please do let me know your thoughts if you do decide to read any of them!</p><span id='bc_0_102MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1554033452'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=8559812186227696209'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_102BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_102B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_104B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_xdaBiG1GPK8/SXqB9s-9lwI/AAAAAAAADqM/SoAZNom8kK4/S45/Joyceintub.1.jpg'></img></div><div id='c1459836658191297796' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_104M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/00242014772865144219'>Joyce</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368471817811#c1459836658191297796'>3:03 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_104MC' class='comment-content'> &quot;a bunch of sarcastic bohemian Canadian people.&quot;<br /><br />Wow, nice characterization. class='comment-content'>Eric Frank Russell...&quot;And then there were none&quot;. <br /><br />MYOB!</p><span id='bc_0_105MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1793402347'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=69200761585131894'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_105BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_105B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_109B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c71068464152563908' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_109M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/09759698345272416750'>Matt</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368478963391#c71068464152563908'>5:02 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_109MC' class='comment-content'>Fantasy with economics:<br /><br />China Mieville has a PhD in International Relations from the London School of Economics. He wrote his thesis on Marxism and international law. He writes brilliant fantasy (and sci-fi) with economic, political, and philosophic themes. Iron Council, for instance, is basically a mythologizing of a labor revolt, plus giant monsters and baleful magicks.<br /><br />Daniel Abraham&#39;s Long Price deals extensively with the political and economic consequences of its system of magic: beings created from thought which embody a specific concept. For instance, in the first novel there is a being called Seedless, which is leveraged to great economic advantage for the province that controls it; it can cause seeds to instantly fall away from a warehouse of cotton bales. And, it is responsible for the &quot;sad trade&quot;, i.e. abortion. These books are elegant, with the strongest characterizations I&#39;ve read in any fantasy, and great emotional heft. I read lots of fantasy, and this series is my favorite.<br /><br />And I second KJ Parker. The Engineer trilogy is basically a how-to manual for dragging a medieval culture into the gunpowder age. Extremely detailed descriptions of everything from the creation of new industries and the economic support they&#39;d need to the craftsmanship and tools necessary to, say, forge cannon. All of her (his?) work is very concerned with exhaustive technological detail (including the economics of technology) in fictional pre-Industrial societies.</p><span id='bc_0_109MN' The civilization in that book is even called the Mercatoria.  </p><span id='bc_0_108MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1659579208'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=7031994933575392538'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_108BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_108B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li></div><div id='bc_0_106I' class='continue' kind='ci'><a href='javascript:;' target='_self'>Reply</a></div></ol><div id='bc_0_106T_box' class='comment-replybox-thread'></div></div></span></div><div id='bc_0_109B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_110B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c6148886147078380286' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_110M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/11868446481195390009'>Robert Carnegie</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368481306403#c6148886147078380286'>5:41 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_110MC' class='comment-content'>Joe Haldeman&#39;s novel &quot;The Long Habit of Living&quot; or &quot;Buying Time&quot; is set around a medical foundation that for many years has been granting millionaires - in British currency - ten years of youth and health in exchange for -all- of their money.  This leads to a core group of people who can make another million in ten years and apparently live forever, and a very, very, very rich foundation, although delivering the treatment isn&#39;t cheap either.  The foundation is represented as a social experiment in getting money out of the hands of the very very rich, but maybe it&#39;s less an economic fable and more a boisterous and violent sci-fi thriller.<br /><br />Elsewhere, a business of selling tailor-made planets to astonishingly wealthy customers meant that Magrathea itself soon became the richest planet of all time and the rest of the Galaxy was reduced to abject poverty. And so the system broke down, the Empire collapsed, and a long sullen silence settled over a billion worlds, disturbed only by the pen scratchings of scholars as they laboured into the night over smug little treaties on the value of a planned political economy.<br />Magrathea itself disappeared and its memory soon passed into the obscurity of legend.<br />In these enlightened days of course, no one believes a word of it, and the myth of Magrathea has become a fairy story that parents tell their kids about at night if they want them to grow up to become economists. <br /><br />If, because one economics graduate was reduced to selling (or rather renting) her body on the street in Han Dold City, with the additional self-humiliation of a very special service for rich johns: she tells them it&#39;s okay to be rich.  Well, some others with her qualifications only do that part.</p><span id='bc_0_110MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-86267419'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=6148886147078380286'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_110BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_110B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_111B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c5172908728562160991' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_111M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/13861479137470362062'>Rusty</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1368490316954#c5172908728562160991'>8:11 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_111MC' class='comment-content'>Another fantasy that has strong economic elements is Daniel Abraham&#39;s Long Price Quartet. id='bc_0_120MC' class='comment-content'>I have to take issue with the OP on Lucifer&#39;s Hammer in that I don&#39;t think the nuclear power plant is being built by an entrepreneur?  I think it is being built by the local power company?<br /><br />SPOILER WARNING<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Like *Inferno* it&#39;s a wish fulfillment book for Pournelle and Niven.  The bad guys become an alliance of cannibals: black ghetto gangsters (led by an ex US Army sergeant), and anti-nuke eco greenies and socialists, desperate to destroy the nuclear power plant.<br /><br />The novel mostly reads like an Irwin Allen disaster movie, and may have been intended to be sold as a film script.  120 pages of &#39;character development&#39; whilst we set up all these characters then destroy the City of Los Angeles.<br /><br />What is very good is the very realistic description of the battle between Senator Jellison&#39;s &#39;good guys&#39; and the eco-greenie-socialist-black-cannibal bad guys.  Pournelle&#39;s military experience really comes through (US Artillery Korea).  Along with David Drake (US Armoured Cavalry, Vietnam) and Glen Cook (infantry Vietnam) and Joe Haldeman (combat engineers Vietnam) Pournelle is one of the best of the SF/ Fantasy community in writing about wars and battles (see also Cyril M. Kornbluth (.50 cal machinegunner, Bulge/ Ardennes).  Oh and JRR Tolkein of course (the trenches, WW1).<br /></p><span id='bc_0_120MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span Apart from, you know, being a harsh barren moon, which is really not the fault of the anarcho-syndicalists.<br /><br />The really interesting thing about The Dispossessed is LeGuin&#39;s insight that a world without property and hierarchy would be very hard to maintain: that it would naturally start degenerating into property and hierarchy very quickly.  Despite indoctrination from birth, the &quot;Syndicate of Initiative&quot; has to go through the process of anarcho-syndicalist revolution against the *property-owning bureaucracy* which has crept up, unbidden, on the purportedly anarcho-syndicalist world...</p><span id='bc_0_125MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-1338132252'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=1132411911270162050'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_125BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_125B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_126B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/openid36-rounded.png'></img></div><div id='c4186299427788673230' class='comment-block'><div id='bc_0_126M' class='comment-header' kind='m'><cite class='user'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://psikeyhackr.livejournal.com/'>psikeyhackr</a></cite><span class='icon user'></span><span class='datetime secondary-text'><a rel='nofollow' href='http://noahpinionblog.blogspot.com/2013/05/science-fiction-for-economists.html?showComment=1370460733548#c4186299427788673230'>3:32 PM</a></span></div><p id='bc_0_126MC' class='comment-content'>We have too much economic philosophy in our economics to justify the economic power games.  Physics does not care.  Cars wear out.  What have Americans lost on the depreciation of cars since the Cuban Missile Crisis?  50 years of forgotten depreciation!  Can economists do algebra?<br /><br />Subversive (1962) by Mack Reynolds<br />http://www.digilibraries.com/ebook/115574/Subversive/<br /><br />Cost of Living (1952) by Robert Sheckley<br />https://senjibqa.wordpress.com/2011/06/17/cost-of-living/<br /><br />The Cosmic Computer (1958) by H. Beam Piper<br />http://librivox.org/the-cosmic-computer-by-h-beam-piper/<br />http://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/20727</p><span id='bc_0_126MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control blog-admin pid-106774151'><a o='d' target='_self' href='http://www.blogger.com/delete-comment.g?blogID=17232051&amp;postID=4186299427788673230'>Delete</a></span></span></div><div id='bc_0_126BR' class='comment-replies'></div><div id='bc_0_126B_box' class='comment-replybox-single'></div></li><li id='bc_0_128B' class='comment' kind='b'><div class='avatar-image-container'><img src='http://img1.blogblog.com/img/anon36.png'></img></div><div id='bc_0_128MC' class='comment-content'>Ken MacLeod&#39;s &quot;Fall Revolution&quot; series is a Marxist take on future societies, but portrays anarchist-syndicalist societies flourishing, while Trotskyist ones on Earth also get by.</p><span id='bc_0_128MN' class='comment-actions secondary-text' kind='m'><a kind='i' href='javascript:;' target='_self' o='r'>Reply</a><span class='item-control
